<table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="151" data-file-width="343" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Arabic_albayancalligraphy.svg/40px-Arabic_albayancalligraphy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Arabic_albayancalligraphy.svg/60px-Arabic_albayancalligraphy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Arabic_albayancalligraphy.svg/80px-Arabic_albayancalligraphy.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Arabic" title="Help:Arabic">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Algeria</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/ɪər/: 'ear' in 'near'">ɪər</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-Algeria-pronunciation.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/16/En-Algeria-pronunciation.ogg" title="En-Algeria-pronunciation.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الجزائر</span>‎ <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language transliteration">al-Jazā'ir</i></i>, familary <a href="/wiki/Algerian_Arabic" title="Algerian Arabic">Algerian Arabic</a> <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" title="Arabic language text">الدزاير</span> <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language transliteration">al-dzāyīr</i></i>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Algérie</i>), officially the <b>People's Democratic Republic of Algeria</b>, is a country in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean coast</a>. The capital and most populous city is <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a>, located in the far north of the country. With an area of 2,381,741 square kilometres (919,595 sq mi), Algeria is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">tenth-largest country in the world</a>, and the largest in Africa since <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> became independent from <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Algeria is bordered to the northeast by <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, to the east by <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, to the west by <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, to the southwest by the <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Saharan</a> territory, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, to the southeast by <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, and to the north by the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. The country is a <a href="/wiki/Semi-presidential_system" title="Semi-presidential system">semi-presidential republic</a> consisting of 48 provinces and 1,541 communes (counties).
</p><p>Ancient Algeria has known many empires and dynasties, including ancient <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punic" title="Punic">Carthaginians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idrisid" title="Idrisid">Idrisid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aghlabid" title="Aghlabid">Aghlabid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rustamid" title="Rustamid">Rustamid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">Fatimids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirid" title="Zirid">Zirid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hammadids" title="Hammadids">Hammadids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almoravids" title="Almoravids">Almoravids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohads" title="Almohads">Almohads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spaniards" title="Spaniards">Spaniards</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottomans" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">French colonial empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> are the indigenous inhabitants of Algeria.
</p><p>Algeria is a <a href="/wiki/Regional_power" title="Regional power">regional</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_power" title="Middle power">middle power</a>. The North African country supplies large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> to Europe, and energy exports are the backbone of the economy. According to <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> Algeria has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_proven_oil_reserves" title="List of countries by proven oil reserves">16th largest</a> oil reserves in the world and the second largest in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, while it has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_proven_reserves" title="List of countries by natural gas proven reserves">9th largest reserves</a> of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sonatrach" title="Sonatrach">Sonatrach</a>, the national oil company, is the largest company in Africa. Algeria has one of the largest militaries in Africa and the largest defence budget on the continent; most of Algeria's weapons are imported from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, with whom they are a close ally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlgeriaSpending_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlgeriaSpending-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> Algeria is a member of the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Maghreb_Union" title="Arab Maghreb Union">Arab Maghreb Union</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The country's name derives from the city of <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a>. The city's name in turn derives from the Arabic <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic language text">al-Jazā'ir</i></i> (<span dir="rtl" lang="ar" title="Arabic language text">الجزائر</span>, "The Islands"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> a truncated form of the older <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic language text">Jazā'ir Banī Mazghanna</i></i> (<span dir="rtl" lang="ar" title="Arabic language text">جزائر بني مزغنة</span>, "Islands of the Mazghanna Tribe"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-بازينة2010_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-بازينة2010-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idrisi_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-idrisi-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2013)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khaldun_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-khaldun-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2013)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> employed by medieval geographers such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Idrisi" title="Muhammad al-Idrisi">al-Idrisi</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Algeria" title="History of Algeria">History of Algeria</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(July 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_North_Africa" title="Prehistoric North Africa">Prehistoric North Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa_during_Antiquity" title="North Africa during Antiquity">North Africa during Antiquity</a></div>
<p>In the region of Ain Hanech (<a href="/wiki/Sa%C3%AFda_Province" title="Saïda Province">Saïda Province</a>), early remnants (200,000 BC) of hominid occupation in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> were found. Neanderthal tool makers produced hand axes in the <a href="/wiki/Levallois_technique" title="Levallois technique">Levalloisian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mousterian" title="Mousterian">Mousterian</a> styles (43,000 BC) similar to those in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Algeria was the site of the highest state of development of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Flake_tool" title="Flake tool">Flake tool</a> techniques. Tools of this era, starting about 30,000 BC, are called <a href="/wiki/Aterian" title="Aterian">Aterian</a> (after the archeological site of <a href="/wiki/Bir_el_Ater" title="Bir el Ater">Bir el Ater</a>, south of <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A9bessa" title="Tébessa">Tebessa</a>).
</p><p>The earliest blade industries in North Africa are called <a href="/wiki/Iberomaurusian" title="Iberomaurusian">Iberomaurusian</a> (located mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Oran" title="Oran">Oran</a> region). This industry appears to have spread throughout the coastal regions of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> between 15,000 and 10,000 BC. Neolithic civilization (animal domestication and agriculture) developed in the Saharan and Mediterranean Maghreb perhaps as early as 11,000 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> or as late as between 6000 and 2000 BC. This life, richly depicted in the <a href="/wiki/Tassili_n%27Ajjer" title="Tassili n'Ajjer">Tassili n'Ajjer</a> paintings, predominated in Algeria until the classical period. The mixture of peoples of North Africa coalesced eventually into a distinct native population that came to be called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berbers</a>, who are the indigenous peoples of northern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg/220px-Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg/330px-Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg/440px-Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dj%C3%A9mila-109038.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman ruins at <a href="/wiki/Dj%C3%A9mila" title="Djémila">Djémila</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timgad_rue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Timgad_rue.jpg/220px-Timgad_rue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Timgad_rue.jpg/330px-Timgad_rue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Timgad_rue.jpg/440px-Timgad_rue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timgad_rue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> ruins of <a href="/wiki/Timgad" title="Timgad">Timgadon</a> the street leading to the local <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Trajan_(Timgad)" title="Arch of Trajan (Timgad)">Arch of Trajan</a></div></div></div>
<p>From their principal center of power at <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthaginians" title="Carthaginians">Carthaginians</a> expanded and established small settlements along the North African coast; by 600 BC, a <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> presence existed at <a href="/wiki/Tipasa" title="Tipasa">Tipasa</a>, east of <a href="/wiki/Cherchell" title="Cherchell">Cherchell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hippo_Regius" title="Hippo Regius">Hippo Regius</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Annaba" title="Annaba">Annaba</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rusicade" title="Rusicade">Rusicade</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Skikda" title="Skikda">Skikda</a>). These settlements served as market towns as well as anchorages.
</p><p>As Carthaginian power grew, its impact on the indigenous population increased dramatically. Berber civilization was already at a stage in which agriculture, manufacturing, trade, and political organization supported several states. Trade links between Carthage and the Berbers in the interior grew, but territorial expansion also resulted in the enslavement or military recruitment of some Berbers and in the extraction of tribute from others.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egypt,_Rome,_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1924" data-file-width="2360" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Egypt%2C_Rome%2C_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg/220px-Egypt%2C_Rome%2C_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Egypt%2C_Rome%2C_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg/330px-Egypt%2C_Rome%2C_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Egypt%2C_Rome%2C_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg/440px-Egypt%2C_Rome%2C_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egypt,_Rome,_Carthage_and_Numidia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Numidia along with Egypt, Rome, and Carthage 200 BC</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1792" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG/220px-Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG/330px-Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG/440px-Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tombeau_de_la_Chr%C3%A9tienne.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Royal_Mausoleum_of_Mauretania" title="Royal Mausoleum of Mauretania">Royal Mausoleum of Mauretania</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> King <a href="/wiki/Juba_II" title="Juba II">Juba II</a> and Queen <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_Selene_II" title="Cleopatra Selene II">Cleopatra Selene II</a>, sovereigns of <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>, are buried</div></div></div>
<p>By the early 4th century BC, Berbers formed the single largest element of the Carthaginian army. In the <a href="/wiki/Mercenary_War" title="Mercenary War">Revolt of the Mercenaries</a>, Berber soldiers rebelled from 241 to 238 BC after being unpaid following the defeat of Carthage in the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>. They succeeded in obtaining control of much of Carthage's North African territory, and they minted coins bearing the name Libyan, used in Greek to describe natives of North Africa. The Carthaginian state declined because of successive defeats by the Romans in the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GM_Massinissa.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="862" data-file-width="865" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/GM_Massinissa.png/180px-GM_Massinissa.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/GM_Massinissa.png/270px-GM_Massinissa.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/GM_Massinissa.png/360px-GM_Massinissa.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GM_Massinissa.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Masinissa" title="Masinissa">Masinissa</a> (c. 238–148 BC), first king of Numidia</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jugurtha.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="184" data-file-width="184" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Jugurtha.JPG/170px-Jugurtha.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/Jugurtha.JPG 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jugurtha.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jugurtha" title="Jugurtha">Jugurtha</a> (c. 160–104 BC), king of Numidia</div></div></div>
<p>In 146 BC the city of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> was destroyed. As Carthaginian power waned, the influence of Berber leaders in the hinterland grew. By the 2nd century BC, several large but loosely administered Berber kingdoms had emerged. Two of them were established in <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a>, behind the coastal areas controlled by Carthage. West of Numidia lay <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>, which extended across the <a href="/wiki/Moulouya_River" title="Moulouya River">Moulouya River</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> to the Atlantic Ocean. The high point of Berber civilization, unequaled until the coming of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohads" title="Almohads">Almohads</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almoravids" title="Almoravids">Almoravids</a> more than a millennium later, was reached during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Masinissa" title="Masinissa">Masinissa</a> in the 2nd century BC.
</p><p>After Masinissa's death in 148 BC, the Berber kingdoms were divided and reunited several times. Masinissa's line survived until 24 AD, when the remaining Berber territory was annexed to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.
</p><p>For several centuries Algeria was ruled by the Romans, who founded many colonies in the region. Like the rest of North Africa, Algeria was one of the breadbaskets of the empire, exporting cereals and other agricultural products. <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Saint Augustine</a> was the bishop of <a href="/wiki/Hippo_Regius" title="Hippo Regius">Hippo Regius</a> (modern-day Algeria), located in the Roman province of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a>. The Germanic <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geiseric" title="Geiseric">Geiseric</a> moved into North Africa in 429, and by 435 controlled coastal Numidia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandaf_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-vandaf-23">[22]</a></sup> They did not make any significant settlement on the land, as they were harassed by local tribes. In fact, by the time the Byzantines arrived Lepcis Magna was abandoned and the Msellata region was occupied by the indigenous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazigh" title="Amazigh">Laguatan</a> who had been busy facilitating an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazigh" title="Amazigh">Amazigh</a> political, military and cultural revival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandaf_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-vandaf-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Muslim_Algeria" title="Medieval Muslim Algeria">Medieval Muslim Algeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mansourah-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Mansourah-1.jpg/220px-Mansourah-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Mansourah-1.jpg/330px-Mansourah-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Mansourah-1.jpg/440px-Mansourah-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mansourah-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mansourah mosque, Tlemcen</div></div></div>
<p>After negligible resistance from the locals, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> conquered Algeria in the mid-7th century and a large number of the indigenous people converted to the newly founded faith of Islam. After the fall of the Umayyad Caliphate, numerous local dynasties emerged, including the <a href="/wiki/Aghlabids" title="Aghlabids">Aghlabids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohads" title="Almohads">Almohads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdalwadid" title="Abdalwadid">Abdalwadid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirids" title="Zirids">Zirids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rustamids" title="Rustamids">Rustamids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hammadids" title="Hammadids">Hammadids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almoravids" title="Almoravids">Almoravids</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimids" title="Fatimids">Fatimids</a>.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, North Africa was home to many great scholars, saints and sovereigns including <a href="/wiki/Judah_ibn_Kuraish" title="Judah ibn Kuraish">Judah Ibn Quraysh</a>, the first grammarian to suggest the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> language family, the great Sufi masters <a href="/wiki/Abu_Madyan" title="Abu Madyan">Sidi Boumediene (Abu Madyan)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sidi_El_Houari" title="Sidi El Houari">Sidi El Houari</a>, and the Emirs <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Mu%27min" title="Abd al-Mu'min">Abd Al Mu'min</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yaghmurasen_Ibn_Zyan" title="Yaghmurasen Ibn Zyan">Yāghmūrasen</a>. It was during this time that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimids" title="Fatimids">Fatimids</a> or children of Fatima, daughter of Muhammad, came to the Maghreb. These "Fatimids" went on to found a long lasting dynasty stretching across the Maghreb, Hejaz and the Levant, boasting a secular inner government, as well as a powerful army and navy, made up primarily of <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levantines</a> extending from Algeria to their capital state of <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid_caliphate" title="Fatimid caliphate">Fatimid caliphate</a> began to collapse when its governors the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirids" title="Zirids">Zirids</a> seceded. In order to punish them the Fatimids sent the Arab <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banu_Sulaym" title="Banu Sulaym">Banu Sulaym</a> against them. The resultant war is recounted in the epic <a href="/wiki/Taghribat_Bani_Hilal" title="Taghribat Bani Hilal">Tāghribāt</a>. In Al-Tāghrībāt the Amazigh Zirid Hero <a href="/wiki/Khalifa_al-Zanati" title="Khalifa al-Zanati">Khālīfā Al-Zānatī</a> asks daily, for duels, to defeat the Hilalan hero <a href="/wiki/Abu_Zayd_al-Hilali" title="Abu Zayd al-Hilali">Ābu Zayd al-Hilalī</a> and many other Arab knights in a string of victories. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirid" title="Zirid">Zirids</a>, however, were ultimately defeated ushering in an adoption of Arab customs and culture. The indigenous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Amazigh</a> tribes, however, remained largely independent, and depending on tribe, location and time controlled varying parts of the Maghreb, at times unifying it (as under the Fatimids). The Fatimid Islamic state, also known as <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> made an Islamic empire that included North Africa, Sicily, <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast of Africa, Tihamah, <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Caliphates from Northern Africa traded with the other empires of their time, as well as forming part of a confederated support and trade network with other Islamic states during the Islamic Era.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="983" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/220px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/330px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/440px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fatimid Caliphate, c. 960–1100</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Amazighs</a> historically consisted of several tribes. The two main branches were the Botr and Barnès tribes, who were divided into tribes, and again into sub-tribes. Each region of the Maghreb contained several tribes (for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanhadja" title="Sanhadja">Sanhadja</a>, <a href="/wiki/Houara" title="Houara">Houara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zenata" title="Zenata">Zenata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Masmuda" title="Masmuda">Masmouda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kutama" title="Kutama">Kutama</a>, Awarba, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berghwata" title="Berghwata">Berghwata</a>). All these tribes made independent territorial decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Amazigh</a> dynasties emerged during the Middle Ages in the Maghreb and other nearby lands. <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> provides a table summarising the Amazigh dynasties of the Maghreb region, the <a href="/wiki/Zirid_dynasty" title="Zirid dynasty">Zirid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banu_Ifran" title="Banu Ifran">Banu Ifran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maghrawa" title="Maghrawa">Maghrawa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almoravid" title="Almoravid">Almoravid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hammadid" title="Hammadid">Hammadid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohad" title="Almohad">Almohad</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merinid" title="Merinid">Merinid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zayyanid_dynasty" title="Zayyanid dynasty">Abdalwadid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wattasid" title="Wattasid">Wattasid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meknassa" title="Meknassa">Meknassa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafsid" title="Hafsid">Hafsid</a> dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> constructed fortified outposts (<a href="/wiki/Presidio" title="Presidio">presidios</a>) on or near the Algerian coast. <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> took control of few coastal towns like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mers_el_Kebir" title="Mers el Kebir">Mers el Kebir</a> in 1505; <a href="/wiki/Oran" title="Oran">Oran</a> in 1509; and <a href="/wiki/Tlemcen" title="Tlemcen">Tlemcen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mostaganem" title="Mostaganem">Mostaganem</a> and <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A9n%C3%A8s" title="Ténès">Ténès</a> in 1510. In the same year, a few merchants of Algiers ceded one of the rocky islets in their harbour to Spain, which built a fort on it. The presidios in North Africa turned out to be a costly and largely ineffective military endeavour that did not guarantee access for Spain's merchant fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_almohade.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="648" data-file-width="882" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Empire_almohade.PNG/220px-Empire_almohade.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Empire_almohade.PNG/330px-Empire_almohade.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Empire_almohade.PNG/440px-Empire_almohade.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_almohade.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Almohad_Caliphate" title="Almohad Caliphate">Almohad Caliphate</a> at its greatest extent, c. 1212</div></div></div>
<p>There reigned in <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">Ifriqiya</a>, current Tunisia, a <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> family, <a href="/wiki/Zirid_dynasty" title="Zirid dynasty">Zirid</a>, somehow recognising the suzerainty of the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid</a> caliph of <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>. Probably in 1048, the Zirid ruler or viceroy, el-Mu'izz, decided to end this suzerainty. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">Fatimid</a> state was too weak to attempt a punitive expedition; The Viceroy, el-Mu'izz, also found another means of revenge.
</p><p>Between the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> were living <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> tribes expelled from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> for their disruption and turbulent influence, both <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banu_Sulaym" title="Banu Sulaym">Banu Sulaym</a> among others, whose presence disrupted farmers in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a> since the nomads would often loot. The then <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid</a> vizier devised to relinquish control of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> and obtained the agreement of his sovereign. This not only prompted the Bedouins to leave, but the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">Fatimid</a> treasury even gave them a light expatriation cash allowance.
</p><p>Whole tribes set off with women, children, ancestors, animals and camping equipment. Some stopped on the way, especially in <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a>, where they are still one of the essential elements of the settlement but most arrived in <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">Ifriqiya</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Gab%C3%A8s_Governorate" title="Gabès Governorate">Gabes</a> region. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirid" title="Zirid">Zirid</a> ruler tried to stop this rising tide, but each meeting, the last under the walls of <a href="/wiki/Kairouan" title="Kairouan">Kairouan</a>, his troops were defeated and Arabs remained masters of the field.
</p><p>The flood was still rising, and in 1057 the Arabs spread on the high plains of <a href="/wiki/Constantine,_Algeria" title="Constantine, Algeria">Constantine</a> where they gradually choked <a href="/wiki/Beni_Hammad_Fort" title="Beni Hammad Fort">Qalaa of Banu Hammad</a>, as they had done Kairouan few decades ago. From there they gradually gained the upper <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oran" title="Oran">Oran</a> plains. Some were forcibly taken by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohads" title="Almohads">Almohads</a> in the second half of the 12th century. We can say that in the 13th century there were in all of <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, with the exception of the main mountain ranges and certain coastal regions remained entirely Berber.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_era">Ottoman era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ottoman era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Algeria" title="Ottoman Algeria">Ottoman Algeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1025" data-file-width="3125" height="72" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg/220px-Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg/330px-Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg/440px-Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magr%C3%A8b_s%C3%A8gle_XV-es.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Zayyanid kingdom of Tlemcen in the fifteenth century and its neighbors</div></div></div>
<p>The region of Algeria was partially ruled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottomans" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a> for three centuries from 1516 to 1830. In 1516 the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a> privateer brothers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aruj" title="Aruj">Aruj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hayreddin_Barbarossa" title="Hayreddin Barbarossa">Hayreddin Barbarossa</a>, who operated successfully under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafsids" title="Hafsids">Hafsids</a>, moved their base of operations to Algiers. They succeeded in conquering Jijel and Algiers from the <a href="/wiki/Spaniards" title="Spaniards">Spaniards</a> but eventually assumed control over the city and the surrounding region, forcing the previous ruler, Abu Hamo Musa III of the <i>Bani Ziyad</i> dynasty, to flee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> When Aruj was killed in 1518 during his invasion of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tlemcen" title="Kingdom of Tlemcen">Tlemcen</a>, Hayreddin succeeded him as military commander of Algiers. The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> sultan gave him the title of <a href="/wiki/Beylerbey" title="Beylerbey">beylerbey</a> and a contingent of some 2,000 <a href="/wiki/Janissaries" title="Janissaries">janissaries</a>. With the aid of this force, Hayreddin conquered the whole area between Constantine and Oran (although the city of Oran remained in Spanish hands until 1791).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csa16_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-csa16-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="787" data-file-width="572" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg/220px-Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg/330px-Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg/440px-Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Barbarossa_Hayreddin_Pasha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hayreddin Barbarossa</div></div></div>
<p>The next beylerbey was Hayreddin's son <a href="/wiki/Hasan_Pasha_(son_of_Barbarossa)" title="Hasan Pasha (son of Barbarossa)">Hasan</a>, who assumed the position in 1544. Until 1587 the area was governed by officers who served terms with no fixed limits. Subsequently, with the institution of a regular Ottoman administration, governors with the title of pasha ruled for three-year terms. The pasha was assisted by janissaries, known in Algeria as the ojaq and led by an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agha_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Agha (Ottoman Empire)">agha</a>. Discontent among the ojaq rose in the mid-1600s because they were not paid regularly, and they repeatedly revolted against the pasha. As a result, the agha charged the pasha with corruption and incompetence and seized power in 1659.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csa16_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-csa16-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">Plague</a> had repeatedly struck the cities of North Africa. Algiers lost from 30,000 to 50,000 inhabitants to the plague in 1620–21, and suffered high fatalities in 1654–57, 1665, 1691 and 1740–42.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1671, the <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">taifa</a> rebelled, killed the agha, and placed one of its own in power. The new leader received the title of <a href="/wiki/Dey" title="Dey">dey</a>. After 1689, the right to select the dey passed to the <a href="/wiki/Divan" title="Divan">divan</a>, a council of some sixty nobles. It was at first dominated by the <i><a href="/wiki/Ojaq" title="Ojaq">ojaq</a></i>; but by the 18th century, it had become the dey's instrument. In 1710, the dey persuaded the sultan to recognise him and his successors as regent, replacing the <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">pasha</a> in that role, although Algiers remained a part of the Ottoman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csa16_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-csa16-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dey was in effect a constitutional autocrat. The dey was elected for a life term, but in the 159 years (1671–1830) that the system survived, fourteen of the twenty-nine deys were assassinated. Despite usurpation, military coups and occasional mob rule, the day-to-day operation of Ottoman government was remarkably orderly. Although the regency patronised the tribal chieftains, it never had the unanimous allegiance of the countryside, where heavy taxation frequently provoked unrest. Autonomous tribal states were tolerated, and the regency's authority was seldom applied in the <a href="/wiki/Kabylie" title="Kabylie">Kabylie</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csa16_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-csa16-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="839" data-file-width="1121" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG/220px-Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG/330px-Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG/440px-Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Debarquement_et_maltraitement_de_prisonniers_a_alger.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Slavery in the Ottoman Empire">Christian slaves</a> in Algiers, 1706</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> preyed on Christian and other non-Islamic shipping in the western Mediterranean Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-33">[32]</a></sup> The pirates often took the passengers and crew on the ships and sold them or used them as <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slaves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> They also did a brisk business in ransoming some of the captives. According to Robert Davis, from the 16th to 19th century, pirates captured 1 million to 1.25 million Europeans as slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barbary_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-barbary-35">[34]</a></sup> They often made raids, called <a href="/wiki/Ghazi_(warrior)" title="Ghazi (warrior)">Razzias</a>, on European coastal towns to capture Christian slaves to sell at <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">slave markets</a> in North Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1544, Hayreddin captured the island of <a href="/wiki/Ischia" title="Ischia">Ischia</a>, taking 4,000 prisoners, and enslaved some 9,000 inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Lipari" title="Lipari">Lipari</a>, almost the entire population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> In 1551, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turgut_Reis" title="Turgut Reis">Turgut Reis</a> enslaved the entire population of the <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Maltese</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Gozo" title="Gozo">Gozo</a>, between 5,000 and 6,000, sending the captives to Libya. In 1554, pirates sacked <a href="/wiki/Vieste" title="Vieste">Vieste</a> in southern Italy and took an estimated 7,000 captives as slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1558, Barbary corsairs captured the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ciutadella" title="Ciutadella">Ciutadella</a> (Minorca), destroyed it, slaughtered the inhabitants and took 3,000 survivors as slaves to <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Barbary pirates often attacked the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>, and in response, the residents built many coastal watchtowers and fortified churches. The threat was so severe that residents abandoned the island of <a href="/wiki/Formentera" title="Formentera">Formentera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Between 1609 and 1616, England lost 466 merchant ships to Barbary pirates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barbary_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-barbary-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:De_Engels-Nederlandse_vloot_in_de_Baai_van_Algiers_ter_ondersteuning_van_het_ultimatum_tot_vrijlating_van_blanke_slaven,_26_augustus_1816._Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1377.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2061" data-file-width="2940" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/De_Engels-Nederlandse_vloot_in_de_Baai_van_Algiers_ter_ondersteuning_van_het_ultimatum_tot_vrijlating_van_blanke_slaven%2C_26_augustus_1816._Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1377.jpeg/220px-thumbnail.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/De_Engels-Nederlandse_vloot_in_de_Baai_van_Algiers_ter_ondersteuning_van_het_ultimatum_tot_vrijlating_van_blanke_slaven%2C_26_augustus_1816._Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1377.jpeg/330px-thumbnail.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/De_Engels-Nederlandse_vloot_in_de_Baai_van_Algiers_ter_ondersteuning_van_het_ultimatum_tot_vrijlating_van_blanke_slaven%2C_26_augustus_1816._Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1377.jpeg/440px-thumbnail.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:De_Engels-Nederlandse_vloot_in_de_Baai_van_Algiers_ter_ondersteuning_van_het_ultimatum_tot_vrijlating_van_blanke_slaven,_26_augustus_1816._Rijksmuseum_SK-A-1377.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bombardment_of_Algiers_(1816)" title="Bombardment of Algiers (1816)">Bombardment of Algiers</a> by the Anglo-Dutch fleet, to support the ultimatum to release European slaves, August 1816</div></div></div>
<p>In July 1627 two pirate ships from Algiers sailed as far as <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Abductions" title="Turkish Abductions">raiding and capturing slaves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Two weeks earlier another pirate ship from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sale,_Morocco" title="Sale, Morocco">Salé</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saadi_dynasty" title="Saadi dynasty">Morocco</a> had also raided in Iceland. Some of the slaves brought to Algiers were later ransomed back to Iceland, but some chose to stay in Algeria. In 1629 pirate ships from Algeria raided the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Barbary raids in the Mediterranean continued to attack Spanish merchant shipping, and as a result, the Spanish navy bombarded Algiers in 1783 and 1784.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mikaberidze_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mikaberidze-47">[46]</a></sup> In 1792, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> abandoned Oran, selling it to the Ottoman Empire, and it became the site for a new bey in Algiers, though French influence in the region increased over the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mikaberidze_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mikaberidze-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 19th century, the pirates forged affiliations with Caribbean powers, paying a "licence tax" in exchange for safe harbour of their vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> One American slave reported that the Algerians had enslaved 130 American seamen in the Mediterranean and Atlantic from 1785 to 1793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Piracy on American vessels in the Mediterranean resulted in the United States initiating the <a href="/wiki/First_Barbary_War" title="First Barbary War">First</a> (1801–1805) and <a href="/wiki/Second_Barbary_War" title="Second Barbary War">Second Barbary Wars</a> (1815). Following those wars, Algeria was weaker and Europeans, with an Anglo-Dutch fleet commanded by the British <a href="/wiki/Edward_Pellew,_1st_Viscount_Exmouth" title="Edward Pellew, 1st Viscount Exmouth">Lord Exmouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombardment_of_Algiers_(1816)" title="Bombardment of Algiers (1816)">attacked Algiers</a>. After a nine-hour bombardment, they obtained a treaty from the Dey that reaffirmed the conditions imposed by Captain (later Commodore) <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Decatur" title="Stephen Decatur">Stephen Decatur</a> (U.S. Navy) concerning the demands of tributes. In addition, the Dey agreed to end the practice of enslaving <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite being removed from Algeria in the 19th century, Spain retained a presence in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>. Algeria consistently opposed Spanish fortresses and control in nearby Morocco through the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mikaberidze_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mikaberidze-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="French_colonization_.281830.E2.80.931962.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="French_colonization_(1830–1962)">French colonization (1830–1962)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: French colonization (1830–1962)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">French Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algerian_War" title="Algerian War">Algerian War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="662" data-file-width="702" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg/220px-Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg/330px-Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg/440px-Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vernet-Combat_de_Somah.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Battle of Somah in 1836</div></div></div>
<p>Under the pretext of a slight to their consul, the French invaded and <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Algiers_in_1830" title="Invasion of Algiers in 1830">captured Algiers</a> in 1830.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Algerian slave trade and piracy ceased when the French conquered Algiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/French_conquest_of_Algeria" title="French conquest of Algeria">conquest of Algeria</a> by the French took some time and resulted in considerable bloodshed. A combination of violence and disease epidemics caused the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> Algerian population to decline by nearly one-third from 1830 to 1872.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ricoux1880_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ricoux1880-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (February 2013)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Ben_Kiernan" title="Ben Kiernan">Ben Kiernan</a> wrote on the French conquest of Algeria: "By 1875, the French conquest was complete. The war had killed approximately 825,000 indigenous Algerians since 1830."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> French losses from 1831–51 were 3,336 killed in action and 92,329 dead in the hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> The population of Algeria, which stood at about 1.5 million in 1830, reached nearly 11 million in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> French policy was predicated on "civilising" the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> During this period, a small but influential French-speaking indigenous elite was formed, made up of Berbers, mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kabyle_People" title="Kabyle People">Kabyles</a>.
As a consequence, French government favored the Kabyles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> About 80% of Indigenous schools were constructed for Kabyles.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abd_al-Qadir.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="392" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Abd_al-Qadir.jpg/150px-Abd_al-Qadir.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Abd_al-Qadir.jpg/225px-Abd_al-Qadir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Abd_al-Qadir.jpg/300px-Abd_al-Qadir.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abd_al-Qadir.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdelkader_El_Djezairi" title="Abdelkader El Djezairi">Emir Abdelkader</a>, Algerian leader insurgent against French colonial rule, 1865</div></div></div>
<p>From 1848 until independence, France administered the whole Mediterranean region of Algeria as an integral part and <i>département</i> of the nation. One of France's longest-held overseas territories, Algeria became a destination for hundreds of thousands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_ethnic_groups" title="European ethnic groups">European</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immigrants" title="Immigrants">immigrants</a>, who became known as <i>colons</i> and later, as <i><a href="/wiki/Pied-Noir" title="Pied-Noir">Pied-Noirs</a>.</i> Between 1825 and 1847, 50,000 French people emigrated to Algeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2013)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> These settlers benefited from the French government's confiscation of communal land from tribal peoples, and the application of modern agricultural techniques that increased the amount of arable land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> Many Europeans settled in <a href="/wiki/Oran" title="Oran">Oran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a>, and by the early 20th century they formed a majority of the population in both cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="500" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg/220px-Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg/330px-Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg/440px-Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Six_chefs_FLN_-_1954.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The six historical Leaders of the FLN: <a href="/wiki/Rabah_Bitat" title="Rabah Bitat">Rabah Bitat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mustapha_Benboula%C3%AFd" title="Mustapha Benboulaïd">Mostefa Ben Boulaïd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Didouche_Mourad" title="Didouche Mourad">Didouche Mourad</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammed_Boudiaf" title="Mohammed Boudiaf">Mohammed Boudiaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krim_Belkacem" title="Krim Belkacem">Krim Belkacem</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Larbi_Ben_M%27Hidi" title="Larbi Ben M'Hidi">Larbi Ben M'Hidi</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>During the late 19th and early 20th century; the European share was almost a fifth of the population. The French government aimed at making Algeria an assimilated part of France, and this included substantial educational investments especially after 1900. The indigenous cultural and religious resistance heavily opposed this tendency, but in contrast to the other colonised countries' path in central Asia and Caucasus, Algeria kept its individual skills and a relatively human-capital intensive agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gradually, dissatisfaction among the Muslim population, which lacked political and economic status in the colonial system, gave rise to demands for greater political autonomy and eventually independence from France. In May 1945, the uprising against the occupying French forces was suppressed through what is now known as the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9tif_and_Guelma_massacre" title="Sétif and Guelma massacre">Sétif and Guelma massacre</a>. Tensions between the two population groups came to a head in 1954, when the first violent events of what was later called the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_War" title="Algerian War">Algerian War</a> began. Historians have estimated that between 30,000 and 150,000 <a href="/wiki/Harki" title="Harki">Harkis</a> and their dependents were killed by the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Front_(Algeria)" title="National Liberation Front (Algeria)">Front de Libération Nationale</a> (FLN) or by <a href="/wiki/Lynching" title="Lynching">lynch</a> mobs in Algeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> The FLN used hit and run attacks in Algeria and France as part of its war, and the French conducted <a href="/wiki/Torture_during_the_Algerian_War_of_Independence" title="Torture during the Algerian War of Independence">severe reprisals</a>.
</p><p>The war led to the death of hundreds of thousands of Algerians and hundreds of thousands of injuries. Historians, like <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Horne" title="Alistair Horne">Alistair Horne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Aron" title="Raymond Aron">Raymond Aron</a>, state that the actual number of Algerian Muslim war dead was far greater than the original FLN and official French estimates but was less than the 1 million deaths claimed by the Algerian government after independence. Horne estimated Algerian casualties during the span of eight years to be around 700,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> The war uprooted more than 2 million Algerians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The war against French rule concluded in 1962, when Algeria gained complete independence following the March 1962 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evian_agreements" title="Evian agreements">Evian agreements</a> and the July 1962 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algerian_self-determination_referendum,_1962" title="Algerian self-determination referendum, 1962">self-determination referendum</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="The_first_three_decades_of_independence_.281962.E2.80.931991.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_first_three_decades_of_independence_(1962–1991)">The first three decades of independence (1962–1991)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The first three decades of independence (1962–1991)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Algeria_(1962%E2%80%9399)" title="History of Algeria (1962–99)">History of Algeria (1962–99)</a></div>
<p>The number of European <i>Pied-Noirs</i> who fled Algeria totaled more than 900,000 between 1962 and 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> The exodus to mainland France accelerated after the <a href="/wiki/Oran_massacre_of_1962" title="Oran massacre of 1962">Oran massacre of 1962</a>, in which hundreds of militants entered European sections of the city, and began attacking civilians.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="414" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg/150px-Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg/225px-Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg/300px-Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Houari_Boumediene%27s_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houari_Boumediene" title="Houari Boumediene">Houari Boumediene</a></div></div></div>
<p>Algeria's first president was the Front de Libération Nationale (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FLN_(Algeria)" title="FLN (Algeria)">FLN</a>) leader <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Ben_Bella" title="Ahmed Ben Bella">Ahmed Ben Bella</a>. Morocco's claim to <a href="/wiki/Greater_Morocco" title="Greater Morocco">portions of western Algeria</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Sand_War" title="Sand War">Sand War</a> in 1963. Ben Bella was overthrown in 1965 by <a href="/wiki/Houari_Boum%C3%A9di%C3%A8ne" title="Houari Boumédiène">Houari Boumédiène</a>, his former ally and defence minister. Under Ben Bella, the government had become increasingly socialist and <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a>; Boumédienne continued this trend. But, he relied much more on the army for his support, and reduced the sole legal party to a symbolic role. He <a href="/wiki/Collective_farming" title="Collective farming">collectivised</a> agriculture and launched a massive industrialization drive. <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">Oil extraction</a> facilities were nationalised. This was especially beneficial to the leadership after the international <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a>.
</p><p>In the 1960s and 1970s under President Houari Boumediene, Algeria pursued a program of industrialization within a state-controlled socialist economy. Boumediene's successor, <a href="/wiki/Chadli_Bendjedid" title="Chadli Bendjedid">Chadli Bendjedid</a>, introduced some liberal economic reforms. He promoted a policy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabisation" title="Arabisation">Arabisation</a> in Algerian society and public life. Teachers of Arabic, brought in from other Muslim countries, spread conventional Islamic thought in schools and sowed the seeds of a return to Orthodox Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faco_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-faco-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Algerian economy became increasingly dependent on oil, leading to hardship when the price collapsed during the <a href="/wiki/1980s_oil_glut" title="1980s oil glut">1980s oil glut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">[69]</a></sup> Economic recession caused by the crash in world oil prices resulted in Algerian social unrest during the 1980s; by the end of the decade, Bendjedid introduced a multi-party system. Political parties developed, such as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Salvation_Front" title="Islamic Salvation Front">Islamic Salvation Front</a> (FIS), a broad coalition of Muslim groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faco_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-faco-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Civil_War_.281991.E2.80.932002.29_and_aftermath"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War_(1991–2002)_and_aftermath">Civil War (1991–2002) and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Civil War (1991–2002) and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Algerian_Civil_War" title="Algerian Civil War">Algerian Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="800" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png/220px-Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png/330px-Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png/440px-Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Algerian_massacres_1997-1998.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/List_of_massacres_during_the_Algerian_Civil_War" title="List of massacres during the Algerian Civil War">Massacres</a> of over 50 people in 1997–1998. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armed_Islamic_Group" title="Armed Islamic Group">Armed Islamic Group (GIA)</a> claimed responsibility for many of them.</div></div></div>
<p>In December 1991 the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Salvation_Front" title="Islamic Salvation Front">Islamic Salvation Front</a> dominated the first of two rounds of <a href="/wiki/Algerian_legislative_election,_1991" title="Algerian legislative election, 1991">legislative elections</a>. Fearing the election of an Islamist government, the authorities intervened on 11 January 1992, cancelling the elections. Bendjedid resigned and a <a href="/wiki/High_Council_of_State_(Algeria)" title="High Council of State (Algeria)">High Council of State</a> was installed to act as Presidency. It banned the FIS, triggering a civil <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurgency</a> between the Front's armed wing, the <a href="/wiki/Armed_Islamic_Group_of_Algeria" title="Armed Islamic Group of Algeria">Armed Islamic Group</a>, and the national armed forces, in which more than 100,000 people are thought to have died. The Islamist militants conducted a violent campaign of <a href="/wiki/List_of_massacres_during_the_Algerian_Civil_War" title="List of massacres during the Algerian Civil War">civilian massacres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> At several points in the conflict, the situation in Algeria became a point of international concern, most notably during the crisis surrounding <a href="/wiki/Air_France_Flight_8969" title="Air France Flight 8969">Air France Flight 8969</a>, a hijacking perpetrated by the Armed Islamic Group. The Armed Islamic Group declared a ceasefire in October 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faco_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-faco-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Algeria held <a href="/wiki/Algerian_presidential_election,_1999" title="Algerian presidential election, 1999">elections in 1999</a>, considered biased by international observers and most opposition groups<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> which were won by President <a href="/wiki/Abdelaziz_Bouteflika" title="Abdelaziz Bouteflika">Abdelaziz Bouteflika</a>. He worked to restore political stability to the country and announced a "Civil Concord" initiative, approved in a <a href="/wiki/Algerian_Civil_Concord_referendum,_1999" title="Algerian Civil Concord referendum, 1999">referendum</a>, under which many political prisoners were pardoned, and several thousand members of armed groups were granted exemption from prosecution under a limited amnesty, in force until 13 January 2000. The AIS disbanded and levels of insurgent violence fell rapidly. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Groupe_Salafiste_pour_la_Pr%C3%A9dication_et_le_Combat" title="Groupe Salafiste pour la Prédication et le Combat">Groupe Salafiste pour la Prédication et le Combat</a> (GSPC), a splinter group of the Group Islamic Army, continued a terrorist campaign against the Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faco_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-faco-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bouteflika was re-elected in the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_presidential_election,_2004" title="Algerian presidential election, 2004">April 2004 presidential election</a> after campaigning on a programme of national reconciliation. The programme comprised economic, institutional, political and social reform to modernise the country, raise living standards, and tackle the causes of alienation. It also included a second amnesty initiative, the <a href="/wiki/Charter_for_Peace_and_National_Reconciliation" title="Charter for Peace and National Reconciliation">Charter for Peace and National Reconciliation</a>, which was approved in a <a href="/wiki/Algerian_national_reconciliation_referendum,_2005" title="Algerian national reconciliation referendum, 2005">referendum in September 2005</a>. It offered amnesty to most guerrillas and Government security forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faco_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-faco-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2008, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algerian_Constitution" title="Algerian Constitution">Algerian Constitution</a> was amended following a vote in Parliament, removing the two-term limit on Presidential incumbents. This change enabled Bouteflika to stand for re-election in the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_presidential_election,_2009" title="Algerian presidential election, 2009">2009 presidential elections</a>, and he was re-elected in April 2009. During his election campaign and following his re-election, Bouteflika promised to extend the programme of national reconciliation and a $150-billion spending programme to create three million new jobs, the construction of one million new housing units, and to continue public sector and infrastructure modernisation programmes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faco_69-5"><a href="#cite_note-faco-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>A continuing series of protests throughout the country started on 28 December 2010, inspired by similar <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">protests across the Middle East and North Africa</a>. On 24 February 2011, the government lifted Algeria's 19-year-old <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> The government enacted legislation dealing with political parties, the electoral code, and the representation of women in elected bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaeo_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaeo-74">[73]</a></sup> In April 2011, Bouteflika promised further constitutional and political reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faco_69-6"><a href="#cite_note-faco-69">[68]</a></sup> However, elections are routinely criticized by opposition groups as unfair and international human rights groups say that media censorship and harassment of political opponents continue.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algeria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
